           CHUNK 10 ANALYSIS
═══════════════════════════════════════

## SECTION 1: DOMAIN & DISCIPLINARY CONTEXT

### 1.1 Primary Academic Domain(s) and Subdomain(s)

1. **Philosophy**: The text primarily falls within the domain of philosophy, specifically within the subdomain of epistemology and logic. It deals with the nature of knowledge, the structure of arguments, and the principles of demonstration.

2. **Logic**: The text is deeply rooted in the study of logic, particularly in the areas of deductive reasoning and syllogistic argumentation. It explores the validity and structure of logical proofs.

### 1.2 Interdisciplinary Connections and Influences

1. **Mathematics**: The text references mathematical concepts such as numbers and geometric shapes (e.g., lines, triangles), indicating a connection to the mathematical sciences. The use of these concepts to illustrate logical principles suggests an interdisciplinary approach.

2. **Natural Sciences**: The discussion of essential and accidental attributes, as well as the necessity of certain connections, hints at a connection to the natural sciences, where understanding the nature of things and their properties is crucial.

### 1.3 Historical Positioning Within the Field

1. **Aristotelian Tradition**: The text aligns with the Aristotelian tradition of logic and epistemology, particularly in its focus on syllogisms and the nature of demonstration. It reflects the historical development of these concepts from Aristotle's works.

2. **Medieval Scholasticism**: The emphasis on essential and accidental attributes, as well as the discussion of necessary and contingent connections, resonates with the concerns of medieval scholastic philosophers who built upon Aristotelian logic.

### 1.4 Relationship to Major Schools of Thought

1. **Rationalism**: The text's focus on the necessity of demonstrative knowledge and the structure of logical proofs aligns with rationalist thought, which emphasizes reason and logical deduction as the primary sources of knowledge.

2. **Empiricism**: While the text does not directly engage with empiricism, its discussion of essential attributes and necessary connections could be seen as a counterpoint to empiricist views that emphasize sensory experience as the basis of knowledge.

### 1.5 Target Audience and Scholarly Community

1. **Philosophers and Logicians**: The primary audience for this text would be philosophers and logicians interested in the nature of demonstration, the structure of arguments, and the principles of logic.

2. **Students of Philosophy and Logic**: The text's detailed analysis of logical structures and definitions of key terms would also be valuable for students studying these subjects.

3. **Scholars of the History of Philosophy**: Given its alignment with the Aristotelian tradition, the text would be of interest to scholars studying the historical development of philosophical thought.

## SECTION 2: MAIN THESIS & CENTRAL ARGUMENTS

### 2.1 Core Thesis Statement

The core thesis of the text is that **demonstration, as the basis of scientific knowledge, must be based on necessary premises and cannot be circular**. This thesis is articulated through the critique of circular demonstration and the emphasis on the necessity of demonstrative knowledge.

### 2.2 Primary Arguments Supporting the Thesis

1. **Critique of Circular Demonstration**:
   - The text argues that circular demonstration, where A implies B and B implies A, leads to the trivial conclusion that if A is, A must be. This is presented as a flawed method of proving anything.
   - It is further argued that circular demonstration is only possible with attributes that imply one another, which are rare in actual demonstrations.

2. **Necessity of Demonstrative Knowledge**:
   - The text asserts that the object of pure scientific knowledge cannot be other than it is, implying that demonstrative knowledge must be based on necessary premises.
   - It is argued that demonstration is an inference from necessary premises, and thus, the premises of demonstration must be characterized by necessity.

3. **Structure of Demonstrative Syllogism**:
   - The text explains that a single term or premise cannot imply a necessary consequent; it takes at least two premises to form a demonstrative syllogism.
   - It is argued that in the first figure of syllogism, it is possible to prove all assumptions on which the original conclusion rested, but this is not possible in other figures.

### 2.3 Secondary Arguments and Subsidiary Claims

1. **Essential and Accidental Attributes**:
   - The text distinguishes between essential and accidental attributes, arguing that essential attributes are necessary and consequentially connected to their subjects.
   - It is claimed that essential attributes are those that are either contained in their subjects or contain their subjects in their defining formulas.

2. **Necessity of Consequential Connections**:
   - The text argues that within the sphere of scientifically known connections, essential attributes are necessary and consequentially connected to their subjects.
   - It is claimed that within a single genus, the contrary of a given attribute is either its privative or its contradictory, and thus, one or the other must inhere in the subject.

### 2.4 Implicit Assumptions Underlying the Arguments

1. **Logical Realism**: The text assumes a realist view of logic, where logical structures and principles are seen as objective and independent of human thought.

2. **Necessity of Scientific Knowledge**: There is an implicit assumption that scientific knowledge must be based on necessary truths, which aligns with a rationalist view of knowledge.

3. **Aristotelian Framework**: The text assumes an Aristotelian framework for understanding logic and demonstration, including the use of syllogisms and the distinction between essential and accidental attributes.

### 2.5 Logical Structure of the Overall Argument

The overall argument is structured as follows:

1. **Introduction of the Problem**: The text begins by critiquing circular demonstration and its limitations.

2. **Establishment of Necessity**: It then establishes the necessity of demonstrative knowledge and the requirement for necessary premises.

3. **Definition of Key Terms**: The text defines essential and accidental attributes, as well as the nature of consequential connections.

4. **Application to Scientific Knowledge**: Finally, it applies these concepts to the sphere of scientifically known connections, arguing for the necessity and consequential nature of essential attributes.

### 2.6 Argument Progression and Development Throughout the Text

1. **Initial Critique**: The text starts with a critique of circular demonstration, setting the stage for the discussion of valid demonstration.

2. **Necessity of Demonstration**: It then moves to the necessity of demonstrative knowledge, establishing the need for necessary premises.

3. **Structure of Syllogism**: The text discusses the structure of the demonstrative syllogism, emphasizing the need for at least two premises.

4. **Definition of Attributes**: It then defines essential and accidental attributes, providing a framework for understanding the nature of scientific knowledge.

5. **Application to Science**: Finally, it applies these concepts to the sphere of scientifically known connections, concluding with the necessity and consequential nature of essential attributes.

## SECTION 3: REPRESENTATIVE QUOTATIONS

1. **Critique of Circular Demonstration**:
   - **Quote**: "Thus by direct proof, if A is, B must be; if B is, C must be; therefore if A is, C must be."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote appears at the beginning of the text, where the author is critiquing the method of circular demonstration.
   - **Significance**: It illustrates the logical structure of a direct proof and sets up the critique of circular demonstration, which is central to the text's argument.

2. **Triviality of Circular Proof**:
   - **Quote**: "Consequently the upholders of circular demonstration are in the position of saying that if A is, A must be- a simple way of proving anything."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote follows the explanation of circular demonstration and its limitations.
   - **Significance**: It highlights the triviality and logical flaw of circular demonstration, reinforcing the text's critique.

3. **Necessity of Demonstrative Knowledge**:
   - **Quote**: "Since the object of pure scientific knowledge cannot be other than it is, the truth obtained by demonstrative knowledge will be necessary."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote appears in the section discussing the necessity of demonstrative knowledge.
   - **Significance**: It establishes the foundational principle that scientific knowledge must be based on necessary truths, which is central to the text's thesis.

4. **Requirement for Necessary Premises**:
   - **Quote**: "And since demonstrative knowledge is only present when we have a demonstration, it follows that demonstration is an inference from necessary premisses."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote follows the discussion of the necessity of demonstrative knowledge.
   - **Significance**: It clarifies that demonstration must be based on necessary premises, reinforcing the text's argument about the nature of scientific knowledge.

5. **Structure of Demonstrative Syllogism**:
   - **Quote**: "Now, it has been shown that the positing of one thing-be it one term or one premiss-never involves a necessary consequent: two premisses constitute the first and smallest foundation for drawing a conclusion at all and therefore a fortiori for the demonstrative syllogism of science."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote appears in the section discussing the structure of the demonstrative syllogism.
   - **Significance**: It explains the logical requirement for at least two premises in a demonstrative syllogism, which is crucial for understanding the text's argument about demonstration.

6. **Definition of Essential Attributes**:
   - **Quote**: "Essential attributes are (1) such as belong to their subject as elements in its essential nature (e.g. line thus belongs to triangle, point to line; for the very being or 'substance' of triangle and line is composed of these elements, which are contained in the formulae defining triangle and line): (2) such that, while they belong to certain subjects, the subjects to which they belong are contained in the attribute's own defining formula."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote appears in the section defining essential and accidental attributes.
   - **Significance**: It provides a detailed definition of essential attributes, which is crucial for understanding the text's argument about the nature of scientific knowledge.

7. **Definition of Accidental Attributes**:
   - **Quote**: "Attributes related in neither of these two ways to their subjects I call accidents or 'coincidents'; e.g. musical or white is a 'coincident' of animal."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote follows the definition of essential attributes.
   - **Significance**: It clarifies the distinction between essential and accidental attributes, which is important for the text's argument about the necessity of essential attributes.

8. **Consequential Connections**:
   - **Quote**: "In another sense again (b) a thing consequentially connected with anything is essential; one not so connected is 'coincidental'."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote appears in the section discussing consequential connections.
   - **Significance**: It introduces the concept of consequential connections, which is central to the text's argument about the necessity of essential attributes.

9. **Necessity of Essential Attributes**:
   - **Quote**: "So far then as concerns the sphere of connexions scientifically known in the unqualified sense of that term, all attributes which (within that sphere) are essential either in the sense that their subjects are contained in them, or in the sense that they are contained in their subjects, are necessary as well as consequentially connected with their subjects."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote appears in the section applying the concepts of essential attributes and consequential connections to scientific knowledge.
   - **Significance**: It reinforces the text's argument that essential attributes are necessary and consequentially connected to their subjects, which is crucial for understanding the nature of scientific knowledge.

10. **Contraries and Necessity**:
    - **Quote**: "For it is impossible for them not to inhere in their subjects either simply or in the qualified sense that one or other of a pair of opposites must inhere in the subject; e.g. in line must be either straightness or curvature, in number either oddness or evenness."
    - **Location/Context**: This quote appears in the section discussing the necessity of essential attributes.

## SECTION 7: CONCEPTUAL APPARATUS

### 7.1 Key Terms and Their Definitions

1. **Demonstration**: Defined as an inference from necessary premises, which is the basis of scientific knowledge.

2. **Essential Attributes**: Attributes that belong to their subject as elements in its essential nature or are contained in the attribute's own defining formula.

3. **Accidental Attributes**: Attributes that are not related to their subjects in the ways essential attributes are, such as musical or white being coincidental to animal.

4. **Consequential Connections**: Connections that are essential because they are causally linked to their subjects, such as the death of a beast being connected to the cutting of its throat.

5. **Coincidental Connections**: Connections that are not causally linked to their subjects, such as lightning occurring while someone is walking.

### 7.2 Technical Vocabulary Introduced or Employed

1. **Syllogism**: A form of deductive argument consisting of two premises and a conclusion.

2. **First Figure**: A specific arrangement of terms in a syllogism, where it is possible to prove all assumptions on which the original conclusion rested.

3. **Privative**: The absence of a quality or attribute, such as not-odd being the privative of odd.

4. **Contradictory**: A pair of opposites where one must be true and the other false, such as odd and even within the genus of number.

### 7.3 Distinctions Drawn Between Related Concepts

1. **Essential vs. Accidental Attributes**: The text distinguishes between attributes that are necessary and inherent to their subjects (essential) and those that are not (accidental).

2. **Consequential vs. Coincidental Connections**: It distinguishes between connections that are essential because they are causally linked to their subjects (consequential) and those that are not (coincidental).

### 7.4 Theoretical Innovations or Neologisms

1. **Commensurate and Universal Attribute**: The text introduces this term to describe attributes that are true in every instance of their subject and at all times.

### 7.5 Repurposing of Existing Concepts

1. **Necessity**: The text repurposes the concept of necessity from its traditional use in logic to apply it to the nature of scientific knowledge and the structure of demonstration.

## SECTION 1: DOMAIN & DISCIPLINARY CONTEXT

### 1.1 Primary Academic Domain(s) and Subdomain(s)

1. **Philosophy**: The text primarily falls within the domain of philosophy, specifically within the subdomain of metaphysics and epistemology. It deals with the nature of attributes and their relationship to subjects, which is a fundamental concern in metaphysics. Additionally, the text's focus on demonstration and proof situates it within epistemology, as it explores how we can know and demonstrate the truth of certain attributes.

2. **Logic**: The text also belongs to the domain of logic, particularly in the subdomain of formal logic and syllogistic reasoning. The author's discussion of commensurately universal attributes and their demonstration involves logical analysis and the structure of arguments.

### 1.2 Interdisciplinary Connections and Influences

1. **Mathematics**: The text's use of geometric examples (e.g., triangles, lines, angles) indicates a strong connection to mathematics. The author employs mathematical concepts to illustrate philosophical points about attributes and their universality.

2. **Science**: The text's emphasis on demonstration and proof suggests a connection to the scientific method, as it deals with how we can establish the truth of certain claims through rigorous argumentation.

### 1.3 Historical Positioning Within the Field

1. **Aristotelian Tradition**: The text aligns with the Aristotelian tradition, particularly in its focus on the nature of attributes and their relationship to subjects. The author's use of terms like "essential" and "commensurately universal" echoes Aristotelian concepts.

2. **Medieval Scholasticism**: The text's detailed analysis of attributes and their demonstration also resonates with the concerns of medieval scholastic philosophers, who often engaged in similar discussions about the nature of universals and their instantiation in particulars.

### 1.4 Relationship to Major Schools of Thought

1. **Realism**: The text's emphasis on the necessity of attributes inhering in their subjects suggests a realist position, as it implies that attributes have an objective existence independent of our perception of them.

2. **Essentialism**: The author's focus on essential attributes and their relationship to subjects aligns with essentialist views, which hold that objects have inherent properties that define their nature.

### 1.5 Target Audience and Scholarly Community

1. **Philosophers**: The primary target audience for this text would be philosophers, particularly those interested in metaphysics, epistemology, and logic. The text's technical language and focus on abstract concepts suggest it is aimed at an academic audience.

2. **Logicians**: The text's discussion of demonstration and proof would also appeal to logicians, who study the structure and validity of arguments.

3. **Mathematicians**: The use of geometric examples and the discussion of mathematical concepts suggest that mathematicians, particularly those interested in the philosophy of mathematics, would find the text relevant.

## SECTION 2: MAIN THESIS & CENTRAL ARGUMENTS

### 2.1 Core Thesis Statement

The core thesis of the text is that attributes must inhere in their subjects of necessity, and that there is a distinction between attributes that are true in every instance and those that are essential. The author further argues that commensurately universal attributes belong to every instance of their subject essentially and as such, and that the demonstration of such attributes is the proof of them as belonging to the first subject commensurately and universally.

### 2.2 Primary Arguments Supporting the Thesis

1. **Necessity of Attributes**: The author argues that attributes must inhere in their subjects of necessity, meaning that if an attribute belongs to a subject, it does so by virtue of the subject's nature. This is supported by the claim that essential attributes and attributes that belong to their subject as such are identical.

   - Example: "attributes must inhere in their subjects of necessity"

2. **Distinction Between True in Every Instance and Essential**: The author distinguishes between attributes that are true in every instance of their subject and those that are essential to the subject. Essential attributes belong to the subject as such, while attributes that are true in every instance may not be essential.

   - Example: "Thus, then, we have established the distinction between the attribute which is 'true in every instance' and the 'essential' attribute."

3. **Commensurately Universal Attributes**: The author defines commensurately universal attributes as those that belong to every instance of their subject essentially and as such. These attributes inhere necessarily in their subjects.

   - Example: "I term 'commensurately universal' an attribute which belongs to every instance of its subject, and to every instance essentially and as such; from which it clearly follows that all commensurate universals inhere necessarily in their subjects."

4. **Demonstration of Commensurately Universal Attributes**: The author argues that the demonstration of a commensurately universal attribute is the proof of it as belonging to the first subject commensurately and universally. This means that the attribute must be shown to belong to any random instance of the subject and that the subject is the first thing to which it can be shown to belong.

   - Example: "An attribute belongs commensurately and universally to a subject when it can be shown to belong to any random instance of that subject and when the subject is the first thing to which it can be shown to belong."

### 2.3 Secondary Arguments and Subsidiary Claims

1. **Examples of Essential Attributes**: The author provides examples of essential attributes, such as point and straight belonging to line, and triangle having two right angles. These examples support the claim that essential attributes belong to their subjects as such.

   - Example: "E.g. point and straight belong to line essentially, for they belong to line as such; and triangle as such has two right angles, for it is essentially equal to two right angles."

2. **Non-Commensurately Universal Attributes**: The author argues that certain attributes, such as the equality of angles to two right angles, are not commensurately universal to certain subjects, such as figure or isosceles triangle. This supports the claim that not all attributes that are true in every instance are commensurately universal.

   - Example: "Thus, e.g. (1) the equality of its angles to two right angles is not a commensurately universal attribute of figure."

3. **Errors in Demonstration**: The author discusses common errors in demonstration, such as when the subject is an individual or individuals above which there is no universal, when the subjects belong to different species and there is a higher universal without a name, and when the subject taken as a whole is really only a part of a larger whole. These errors support the claim that demonstrations must be of the first subject to which the attribute belongs commensurately and universally.

   - Example: "We must not fail to observe that we often fall into error because our conclusion is not in fact primary and commensurately universal in the sense in which we think we prove it so."

### 2.4 Implicit Assumptions Underlying the Arguments

1. **Realism**: The author assumes a realist position, as the text implies that attributes have an objective existence independent of our perception of them.

2. **Essentialism**: The author assumes an essentialist view, as the text focuses on essential attributes and their relationship to subjects.

3. **Logical Structure**: The author assumes that arguments can be structured logically and that demonstrations can be used to establish the truth of certain claims.

### 2.5 Logical Structure of the Overall Argument

The overall argument follows a deductive structure, with the author presenting a series of premises and drawing conclusions from them. The main premises are:

1. Attributes must inhere in their subjects of necessity.
2. There is a distinction between attributes that are true in every instance and those that are essential.
3. Commensurately universal attributes belong to every instance of their subject essentially and as such.
4. The demonstration of a commensurately universal attribute is the proof of it as belonging to the first subject commensurately and universally.

From these premises, the author draws the conclusion that the demonstration of an attribute as belonging to a subject commensurately and universally is the essential sense of demonstration, while the proof of it as belonging to other subjects is demonstration only in a secondary and unessential sense.

### 2.6 Argument Progression and Development Throughout the Text

The argument progresses from the initial claim about the necessity of attributes to the distinction between true in every instance and essential attributes. The author then introduces the concept of commensurately universal attributes and their relationship to demonstration. The argument develops further by providing examples of essential attributes and non-commensurately universal attributes, and by discussing common errors in demonstration. The text concludes by emphasizing the importance of demonstrating attributes as belonging to the first subject commensurately and universally.

## SECTION 2: MAIN THESIS & CENTRAL ARGUMENTS

### 2.1 Core Thesis Statement

The core thesis of the text is that demonstrative knowledge must be based on necessary truths and that the premises of a demonstrative syllogism must be essential and necessary connections.

- **Formulation 1**: "Demonstrative knowledge must rest on necessary basic truths; for the object of scientific knowledge cannot be other than it is."
- **Formulation 2**: "Premisses of the demonstrative syllogism must be connexions essential in the sense explained: for all attributes must inhere essentially or else be accidental, and accidental attributes are not necessary to their subjects."

### 2.2 Primary Arguments Supporting the Thesis

1. **Necessity of Basic Truths**: The text argues that demonstrative knowledge must be based on necessary truths because the object of scientific knowledge cannot be other than it is.
   - This argument is supported by the claim that essential attributes attach necessarily to their subjects, either as elements in the essential nature of the subjects or containing the subjects as elements in their own essential nature.

2. **Essential vs. Accidental Attributes**: The text distinguishes between essential and accidental attributes, asserting that only essential attributes can serve as the basis for demonstrative knowledge.
   - Essential attributes are necessary because they are inherent to the nature of the subject, whereas accidental attributes are contingent and cannot form the basis of necessary conclusions.

3. **Necessity of Premises in Demonstrative Syllogisms**: The text argues that the premises of a demonstrative syllogism must be necessary connections to ensure the necessity of the conclusion.
   - This is supported by the claim that if the middle term of a syllogism is not necessarily connected to the other terms, the conclusion cannot be considered demonstrative knowledge.

4. **Critique of Non-Necessary Premises**: The text critiques the use of non-necessary premises in demonstrative arguments, arguing that such premises cannot lead to genuine scientific knowledge.
   - This is illustrated by the example of a syllogism where the middle term is not necessarily connected to the other terms, leading to a conclusion that is not demonstrative knowledge.

### 2.3 Secondary Arguments and Subsidiary Claims

1. **Role of the Middle Term**: The text argues that the middle term in a demonstrative syllogism must be necessary to ensure the necessity of the conclusion.
   - This is a subsidiary claim that supports the primary argument about the necessity of premises in demonstrative syllogisms.

2. **Critique of Popular Acceptance**: The text critiques the idea that popular acceptance or rejection can serve as a criterion for basic truths in demonstration.
   - This is a secondary argument that supports the primary thesis by emphasizing the need for necessary truths rather than popular opinion.

3. **Necessity of the Conclusion**: The text argues that the conclusion of a demonstration must be necessary and cannot be other than it is.
   - This is a subsidiary claim that supports the primary argument about the nature of demonstrative knowledge.

### 2.4 Implicit Assumptions Underlying the Arguments

1. **Realism**: The text assumes a realist position, where there are objective truths and necessary connections in the world that can be known through demonstration.

2. **Essentialism**: The text assumes an essentialist view of attributes, where certain properties are inherent to the nature of their subjects and cannot be otherwise.

3. **Logical Structure of Knowledge**: The text assumes that knowledge can be structured logically and that demonstrative knowledge follows a specific logical form.

### 2.5 Logical Structure of the Overall Argument

The overall argument follows a deductive structure, starting from the necessity of basic truths and essential attributes and moving to the conclusion that demonstrative knowledge must be based on necessary premises.

- **Premise 1**: Demonstrative knowledge must rest on necessary basic truths.
- **Premise 2**: Essential attributes attach necessarily to their subjects.
- **Premise 3**: Premises of a demonstrative syllogism must be essential and necessary connections.
- **Conclusion**: Demonstrative knowledge must be based on necessary premises.

### 2.6 Argument Progression and Development Throughout the Text

1. **Introduction of the Thesis**: The text begins by introducing the thesis that demonstrative knowledge must be based on necessary truths.

2. **Explanation of Essential Attributes**: The text then explains the concept of essential attributes and their necessary connection to their subjects.

3. **Application to Demonstrative Syllogisms**: The text applies the concept of essential attributes to the premises of demonstrative syllogisms, arguing that only necessary connections can serve as premises.

4. **Critique of Non-Necessary Premises**: The text critiques the use of non-necessary premises in demonstrative arguments, using examples to illustrate the point.

5. **Conclusion and Implications**: The text concludes by reiterating the necessity of necessary premises for demonstrative knowledge and discussing the implications for scientific knowledge.

   - **Location/Context**: This quote appears in the section discussing the role of the middle term in demonstrative syllogisms.

7. **Further Support for the Main Thesis**

   - **Location/Context**: This quote appears in the section discussing the necessity of premises in demonstrative syllogisms.
   - **Significance**: This quote provides further support for the main thesis by emphasizing the distinctive character of demonstration as relying on necessary premises.

8. **Critique of Non-Necessary Premises**
   - **Quote**: "Even if the link has not actually perished but is liable to perish, this situation is possible and might occur. But such a condition cannot be knowledge."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote appears in the section critiquing the use of non-necessary premises in demonstrative arguments.
   - **Significance**: This quote critiques the use of non-necessary premises by illustrating the contingency of such premises and their inability to form the basis of genuine knowledge.

9. **Necessity of the Conclusion**
   - **Quote**: "When the conclusion is necessary, the middle through which it was proved may yet quite easily be non-necessary. You can in fact infer the necessary even from a non-necessary premiss, just as you can infer the true from the not true."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote appears in the section discussing the necessity of the conclusion in demonstrative syllogisms.
   - **Significance**: This quote addresses the relationship between the necessity of the conclusion and the necessity of the premises, highlighting the possibility of inferring necessary conclusions from non-necessary premises.

10. **Implications for Scientific Knowledge**

    - **Location/Context**: This quote appears in the section discussing the implications of the main thesis for scientific knowledge.

1. **Critique of Popular Acceptance**: The text anticipates the objection that popular acceptance can serve as a criterion for basic truths and responds by critiquing the sophists' assumption that to know is the same as to possess knowledge.

2. **Critique of Non-Necessary Premises**: The text anticipates the objection that non-necessary premises can lead to demonstrative knowledge and responds by showing that such premises cannot lead to genuine scientific knowledge.

2. **Critique of Non-Necessary Premises**: The text critiques the use of non-necessary premises in demonstrative arguments, using examples and thought experiments to illustrate the point and strengthen its own argument.

## SECTION 2: MAIN THESIS & CENTRAL ARGUMENTS

### 2.1 Core Thesis Statement
1. **Primary Formulation**: The core thesis of the text is that scientific knowledge and demonstration must be based on essential attributes and appropriate basic truths within a specific genus, and that demonstration cannot cross from one genus to another without certain conditions being met.
2. **Secondary Formulation**: The text also asserts that knowledge of perishable things cannot be strictly scientific because their attributes are not commensurately universal.

### 2.2 Primary Arguments Supporting the Thesis
1. **Essential Attributes and Necessity**: The text argues that only essential attributes within a genus are necessary, and thus, the premises and conclusions of demonstrations that produce scientific knowledge must be essential. This is supported by the claim that accidents are not necessary and do not lead to reasoned knowledge.
   - **Explanation**: The text posits that scientific knowledge requires a demonstration based on essential attributes because only these attributes are necessary and universal within their respective genera. Accidents, being non-essential and non-necessary, cannot form the basis of scientific knowledge.

2. **Demonstration Within a Genus**: The text argues that demonstration cannot pass from one genus to another, using the example of arithmetic and geometry to illustrate this point.
   - **Explanation**: The text explains that each science has its own genus and that demonstration must stay within that genus to be valid. The impossibility of proving geometrical truths by arithmetic unless the magnitudes are numbers is used to support this argument.

3. **Commensurately Universal Premises**: The text argues that for a conclusion to be eternal and thus part of scientific knowledge, the premises from which it is drawn must be commensurately universal.
   - **Explanation**: The text asserts that only premises that are universally true within their genus can lead to conclusions that are part of scientific knowledge. This is because perishable things have attributes that are not commensurately universal, and thus, knowledge of them cannot be strictly scientific.

4. **Appropriate Basic Truths**: The text argues that demonstration must be based on 'appropriate' basic truths that are essential to the subject in question.
   - **Explanation**: The text emphasizes that only premises that are essential and appropriate to the subject can lead to knowledge of an attribute as inhering essentially in that subject. This is contrasted with proofs that use common characters, which can only lead to accidental knowledge.

### 2.3 Secondary Arguments and Subsidiary Claims
1. **Subordinate and Superior Sciences**: The text claims that theorems of one science can be demonstrated by another only if they are related as subordinate to superior.
   - **Explanation**: This claim is used to explain how certain theorems in optics or harmonics can be demonstrated by geometry or arithmetic, respectively, due to their subordinate relationship.

2. **Eternal Nature of Scientific Knowledge**: The text claims that scientific knowledge must be eternal because it is based on commensurately universal premises.
   - **Explanation**: This claim is used to argue that knowledge of perishable things cannot be strictly scientific because their attributes are not eternal.

3. **Indemonstrability of Basic Truths**: The text claims that the basic truths of each inhering attribute are indemonstrable.
   - **Explanation**: This claim is used to support the argument that scientific knowledge must be based on essential and appropriate premises, as these premises are the foundation of demonstration.

### 2.4 Implicit Assumptions Underlying the Arguments
1. **Essentialism**: The text assumes an essentialist view of attributes, where certain properties are necessary and inherent to their subjects.
2. **Realism**: The text assumes a realist view of knowledge, where scientific knowledge corresponds to an objective reality.
3. **Logical Structure of Knowledge**: The text assumes that knowledge can be structured logically and that this structure is essential to understanding the nature of scientific knowledge.

### 2.5 Logical Structure of the Overall Argument
1. **Premises**: The text begins with premises about the nature of essential attributes and the necessity of demonstration within a specific genus.
2. **Intermediate Conclusions**: From these premises, the text derives intermediate conclusions about the impossibility of cross-genus demonstration and the requirement for commensurately universal premises.
3. **Final Conclusion**: The final conclusion is that scientific knowledge must be based on essential attributes and appropriate basic truths within a specific genus, and that knowledge of perishable things cannot be strictly scientific.

### 2.6 Argument Progression and Development Throughout the Text
1. **Introduction of Essential Attributes**: The text begins by introducing the concept of essential attributes and their necessity for scientific knowledge.
2. **Demonstration Within a Genus**: The text then develops the argument that demonstration must stay within a specific genus, using examples from arithmetic and geometry.
3. **Commensurately Universal Premises**: The text further develops the argument by discussing the requirement for commensurately universal premises and the implications for knowledge of perishable things.
4. **Appropriate Basic Truths**: The text concludes by emphasizing the need for demonstration to be based on appropriate basic truths, contrasting this with proofs that use common characters.

## SECTION 3: REPRESENTATIVE QUOTATIONS (Minimum 10 quotations)

1. **Quote**: "Since it is just those attributes within every genus which are essential and possessed by their respective subjects as such that are necessary it is clear that both the conclusions and the premisses of demonstrations which produce scientific knowledge are essential."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote appears at the beginning of the text, setting the stage for the discussion of essential attributes and their role in scientific knowledge.
   - **Significance**: This quote encapsulates the core thesis of the text, emphasizing the necessity of essential attributes for scientific knowledge.

2. **Quote**: "For accidents are not necessary: and, further, since accidents are not necessary one does not necessarily have reasoned knowledge of a conclusion drawn from them."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote follows the introduction of essential attributes, contrasting them with accidents.
   - **Significance**: This quote supports the argument that only essential attributes can form the basis of scientific knowledge, as accidents are not necessary and do not lead to reasoned knowledge.

3. **Quote**: "We cannot, for instance, prove geometrical truths by arithmetic."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote appears in the section discussing the impossibility of cross-genus demonstration.
   - **Significance**: This quote illustrates the text's argument that demonstration must stay within a specific genus, using the example of arithmetic and geometry.

4. **Quote**: "The axioms which are premisses of demonstration may be identical in two or more sciences: but in the case of two different genera such as arithmetic and geometry you cannot apply arithmetical demonstration to the properties of magnitudes unless the magnitudes in question are numbers."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote follows the discussion of the impossibility of cross-genus demonstration, providing further explanation.
   - **Significance**: This quote clarifies the conditions under which demonstration can cross from one genus to another, emphasizing the need for the subject to be of the same kind.

5. **Quote**: "If the premisses from which the syllogism proceeds are commensurately universal, the conclusion of such i.e. in the unqualified sense-must also be eternal."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote appears in the section discussing the requirement for commensurately universal premises.
   - **Significance**: This quote supports the argument that scientific knowledge must be eternal, as it is based on commensurately universal premises.

6. **Quote**: "Therefore no attribute can be demonstrated nor known by strictly scientific knowledge to inhere in perishable things."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote follows the discussion of commensurately universal premises, applying it to perishable things.
   - **Significance**: This quote encapsulates the text's argument that knowledge of perishable things cannot be strictly scientific, as their attributes are not commensurately universal.

7. **Quote**: "The proof can only be accidental, because the attribute's connexion with its perishable subject is not commensurately universal but temporary and special."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote further explains why knowledge of perishable things cannot be strictly scientific.
   - **Significance**: This quote supports the argument that knowledge of perishable things is only accidental, as their attributes are not universally connected to their subjects.

8. **Quote**: "If such a demonstration is made, one premiss must be perishable and not commensurately universal."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote continues the discussion of the limitations of knowledge about perishable things.
   - **Significance**: This quote clarifies the conditions under which a demonstration about perishable things can be made, emphasizing the non-universal nature of the premises.

9. **Quote**: "It is clear that if the conclusion is to show an attribute inhering as such, nothing can be demonstrated except from its 'appropriate' basic truths."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote appears in the section discussing the need for demonstration to be based on appropriate basic truths.
   - **Significance**: This quote encapsulates the text's argument that demonstration must be based on premises that are essential and appropriate to the subject in question.

10. **Quote**: "Consequently a proof even from true, indemonstrable, and immediate premisses does not constitute knowledge."
    - **Location/Context**: This quote follows the discussion of appropriate basic truths, contrasting them with proofs that use common characters.
    - **Significance**: This quote supports the argument that only proofs based on essential and appropriate premises can lead to scientific knowledge, as other proofs can only lead to accidental knowledge.

## SECTION 4: ANALYTICAL MOVES DEPLOYED

### 4.1 Conceptual Analysis and Distinctions Drawn
1. **Essential vs. Accidental Attributes**: The text draws a clear distinction between essential attributes, which are necessary and inherent to their subjects, and accidental attributes, which are not necessary and do not lead to reasoned knowledge.
   - **Explanation**: This distinction is crucial to the text's argument that scientific knowledge must be based on essential attributes. It appears throughout the text, particularly in the sections discussing the necessity of essential attributes for demonstration.
   - **Function**: This distinction serves to clarify the nature of scientific knowledge and the requirements for demonstration, emphasizing the importance of essential attributes.

2. **Commensurately Universal vs. Non-Universal Premises**: The text distinguishes between premises that are commensurately universal, meaning they are universally true within their genus, and premises that are not commensurately universal.
   - **Explanation**: This distinction is used to argue that only premises that are commensurately universal can lead to eternal and thus scientific knowledge. It appears in the sections discussing the requirement for commensurately universal premises.
   - **Function**: This distinction supports the argument that knowledge of perishable things cannot be strictly scientific, as their attributes are not commensurately universal.

3. **Appropriate vs. Inappropriate Basic Truths**: The text distinguishes between basic truths that are appropriate to the subject in question and those that are not.
   - **Explanation**: This distinction is used to argue that demonstration must be based on premises that are essential and appropriate to the subject. It appears in the sections discussing the need for appropriate basic truths.
   - **Function**: This distinction emphasizes the importance of using premises that are relevant and essential to the subject in order to achieve scientific knowledge.

### 4.2 Argumentation Strategies
1. **Reductio ad Absurdum**: The text uses reductio ad absurdum to argue against the possibility of cross-genus demonstration.
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that if it were possible to prove geometrical truths by arithmetic, it would lead to absurd conclusions, such as proving the properties of magnitudes that are not numbers.
   - **Function**: This strategy is used to support the argument that demonstration must stay within a specific genus, as cross-genus demonstration would lead to invalid conclusions.

2. **Modus Tollens**: The text uses modus tollens to argue that if accidents are not necessary, then knowledge based on accidents cannot be reasoned knowledge.
   - **Explanation**: The text states that accidents are not necessary, and therefore, knowledge based on accidents cannot be reasoned knowledge. This is a classic example of modus tollens, where the denial of the consequent leads to the denial of the antecedent.
   - **Function**: This strategy supports the argument that only essential attributes can form the basis of scientific knowledge, as accidents do not lead to reasoned knowledge.

### 4.3 Category Error Detection
1. **Cross-Genus Demonstration**: The text detects a category error in the attempt to demonstrate theorems from one genus using the methods of another.
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that attempting to prove geometrical truths by arithmetic is a category error, as these are different genera with different essential attributes.
   - **Function**: This detection of a category error supports the argument that demonstration must stay within a specific genus, as cross-genus demonstration would involve a fundamental mistake in reasoning.

## SECTION 7: CONCEPTUAL APPARATUS

### 7.1 Key Terms and Their Definitions
1. **Essential Attributes**: Attributes that are necessary and inherent to their subjects, and thus, form the basis of scientific knowledge.
2. **Accidental Attributes**: Attributes that are not necessary and do not lead to reasoned knowledge.
3. **Commensurately Universal Premises**: Premises that are universally true within their genus, and thus, lead to eternal and scientific knowledge.
4. **Appropriate Basic Truths**: Premises that are essential and appropriate to the subject in question, and thus, form the basis of demonstration.

### 7.2 Technical Vocabulary Introduced or Employed
1. **Genus**: A category of subjects with their own essential attributes.
2. **Demonstration**: A logical argument that leads to scientific knowledge, based on essential attributes and appropriate basic truths.
3. **Syllogism**: A form of deductive reasoning that consists of a major premise, a minor premise, and a conclusion.

### 7.3 Distinctions Drawn Between Related Concepts
1. **Essential vs. Accidental Attributes**: The text distinguishes between essential attributes, which are necessary and inherent to their subjects, and accidental attributes, which are not necessary and do not lead to reasoned knowledge.
2. **Commensurately Universal vs. Non-Universal Premises**: The text distinguishes between premises that are commensurately universal, meaning they are universally true within their genus, and premises that are not commensurately universal.
3. **Appropriate vs. Inappropriate Basic Truths**: The text distinguishes between basic truths that are appropriate to the subject in question and those that are not.

### 7.4 Theoretical Innovations or Neologisms
1. **Commensurately Universal**: The text introduces the concept of commensurately universal premises, which are premises that are universally true within their genus and lead to eternal and scientific knowledge.
2. **Appropriate Basic Truths**: The text introduces the concept of appropriate basic truths, which are premises that are essential and appropriate to the subject in question and form the basis of demonstration.

### 7.5 Repurposing of Existing Concepts
